  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_26fe2b0b-66a1-4f9a-b236-2f812edcaa25'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14074226788852510267">Twenty years ago, a boy named Jack Sawyer travelled to a parallel universe called The Territories to save his mother and her Territories "twinner" from a premature and agonizing death that would have brought cataclysm to the other world. Now Jack is a retired Los Angeles homicide detective living in the nearly nonexistent hamlet of Tamarack, WI. He has no recollection of h</span>
  <span id="freeText14074226788852510267" style="display:none">Twenty years ago, a boy named Jack Sawyer travelled to a parallel universe called The Territories to save his mother and her Territories "twinner" from a premature and agonizing death that would have brought cataclysm to the other world. Now Jack is a retired Los Angeles homicide detective living in the nearly nonexistent hamlet of Tamarack, WI. He has no recollection of his adventures in the Territories and was compelled to leave the police force when an odd, happenstance event threatened to awaken those memories.<br /><br />When a series of gruesome murders occur in western Wisconsin that are reminiscent of those committed several decades earlier by a real-life madman named Albert Fish, the killer is dubbed "The Fisherman" and Jack's buddy, the local chief of police, begs Jack to help his inexperienced force find him. But is this merely the work of a disturbed individual, or has a mysterious and malignant force been unleashed in this quiet town? What causes Jack's inexplicable waking dreams, if that is what they are, of robins' eggs and red feathers? It's almost as if someone is trying to tell him something. As that message becomes increasingly impossible to ignore, Jack is drawn back to the Territories and to his own hidden past, where he may find the soul-strength to enter a terrifying house at the end of a deserted track of forest, there to encounter the obscene and ferocious evils sheltered within it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14074226788852510267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_144938'><a id="like_count_community_question_144938" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/144938?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">4 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F10607.Black_House">like</a></span>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/44254542-simina">Simina</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I actually read this before The Talisman because that's the order in which the publishing house translated them, beats me why. So I read The Talisman <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I actually read this before The Talisman because that's the order in which the publishing house translated them, beats me why. So I read The Talisman after and made a lot of new sense but I still got most of it reading this before that. I do advice you read The Talisman before, not only because you'll understand more of BH, but because The Talisman is an amazing read as well, one of King's finest books.<br />Long days and pleasant nights to you!<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/890997-does-anyone-know-exactly-where-black-house">Does anyone know exactly where Black House falls into place in the 
Dark Tower series?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_890997'><a id="like_count_community_question_890997" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/890997?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F10607.Black_House">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/890997-does-anyone-know-exactly-where-black-house">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/890997-does-anyone-know-exactly-where-black-house">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/18279366-rachel"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1500406122i/18279366._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/18279366-rachel">Rachel</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Hey! This might not be the info you're looking for, but I ended up reading Black House between Song of Susannah and The Dark Tower, since I had read T<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Hey! This might not be the info you're looking for, but I ended up reading Black House between Song of Susannah and The Dark Tower, since I had read The Talisman earlier &amp; couldn't get my hands on The Dark Tower right away, and I think it ended up adding a lot to the reading experience of the series actually? I think that there are three main reasons for that: <br /><br />1. chronologically, BH exists between SoS and TDT, so it fit in nicely<br />2. this book has the same sort of conceptual themes &amp; connections that the whole last half of the DT series has, so it didn't really interrupt the flow of the series for me<br />3. it added a lot of world-building, which I always really enjoy in a series, but which I think it was best to have before the final book, since it's SUCH a good finale and I wouldn't have wanted to add anything after that, and also because it adds to the overall sense of epic-ness the whole series builds up to<br /><br />Hope this can help! Happy reading :)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/539127.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320067602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9583014713148674206">"What you love, you must love all the harder because someday it will be gone."<br/><br/>Twenty years ago, a young boy named Jack Sawyer travelled to a parallel universe called The Territories to save his mother's life. Now a retired homicide detective, Jack has no memory of these adventures. There is a sick serial killer on the loose, murdering and eating children, and the local chief of police begs Jack to help his force catch him.<br/><br/>I love Stephen King. I love serial killers. Combine the two and you have a</span>
  <span id="freeText9583014713148674206" style="display:none">"What you love, you must love all the harder because someday it will be gone."<br /><br />Twenty years ago, a young boy named Jack Sawyer travelled to a parallel universe called The Territories to save his mother's life. Now a retired homicide detective, Jack has no memory of these adventures. There is a sick serial killer on the loose, murdering and eating children, and the local chief of police begs Jack to help his force catch him.<br /><br />I love Stephen King. I love serial killers. Combine the two and you have a book that is right up jobis89's street. The Talisman is an epic fantasy tale, spanning across our world and The Territories, as Jack Sawyer embarks on a mission to travel across the country to try and save his mother (and her Twinner) from death. Black House, however, is darker, much more of a horror novel, I feel, and it focuses on one small town - Coulee County. The Talisman is a great, great book, but Black House is just more to my slightly darker, slightly more murderous, cannibalistic tastes… sorrynotsorry *evil laugh*<br /><br />There's so much to love about Black House. Other reviews I had looked at had pointed out the narrative style as something they did not like about Black House - well, I LOVED the narrative style. It's a bit slow and hard to get into at the beginning, but once I did I really enjoyed it. The narrative style is as if you're flying over Coulee County and you're allowed little snapshots into the lives of the residents through a bird's-eye perspective.<br /><br />Yet more awesome characters… we had Wolf in The Talisman, and now we have Henry Leyden in Black House - a blind guy who has a keen ear for voices and sounds. I loved Henry so much!! And the bikers were cool additions too. I generally really liked a lot of the characters in this one. The villain in particular - The Fisherman - was fucking terrifying. King's villains can sometimes be pretty "grey", they aren't always definitively evil. However, in this case, he is evil incarnate, which is no surprise given his similarities to the vile Albert Fish (I could say more but don't want to give away potential spoilers). <br /><br />I had complaints while reading The Talisman that it felt like the Dark Tower without actually being the Dark Tower - almost like a cheap imitation I guess. And finally in Black House my suspicions are answered as connections to the Dark Tower are made. Meanwhile I'm fangirling and getting all emotional over mentions of Roland Deschain and the rest of our beloved ka-tet. Link a book to the Dark Tower series and I. WILL. LOVE. IT. (Well, apart from Insomnia… I did love the connections, it just didn't save the rest of the book!)<br /><br />As most Constant Readers will know, choosing your top 10 King books is pretty much possible, but I do believe this has now earned a spot in my top 10. It might not be for everyone, but it was really to my taste. It's difficult to review books you really loved, so I guess I'll wrap up here… 5 stars!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9583014713148674206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_478725233" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/478725233" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/478725233?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer478725233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6254942034917126324">I hate that the first thing you see of a review is the number of stars it's given. <br/><br/>Someone's feeling about a book is not easily reduced to a five-point scale. And even once that is done, how do I know what five stars means to you? How do you know what five stars means to me? <br/><br/>For me, a five star book is a book that I believe is worth the time and energy you're going to spend reading it. <br/><br/>If, (and this is key) you're into that sort of book. (Horror, Mystery, Fantasy, Hardcore Gothic Gypsy Steampunk</span>
  <span id="freeText6254942034917126324" style="display:none">I hate that the first thing you see of a review is the number of stars it's given. <br /><br />Someone's feeling about a book is not easily reduced to a five-point scale. And even once that is done, how do I know what five stars means to you? How do you know what five stars means to me? <br /><br />For me, a five star book is a book that I believe is worth the time and energy you're going to spend reading it. <br /><br />If, (and this is key) you're into that sort of book. (Horror, Mystery, Fantasy, Hardcore Gothic Gypsy Steampunk.) <br /><br />A six-star book, is a book that I believe is worth your time and energy even if it's *not* the sort of thing you're into. (Generally speaking, this is the sort of book I'll give a promotional blurb for.) <br /><br />Unfortunately, there isn't a six star option here on goodreads. <br /><br />Generally speaking, a four star book is one that irritates me or disappoints me in one or two moderate ways. <br /><br />A three star book has several moderate irritations, or one big one, or or something that was irritating all the way through. <br /><br />Keep in mind that I can be extraordinarily critical of my books. Things that irritate me might not ever even show up on your mental radar. <br /><br />Further complicating things is the fact that sometimes I'm willing to give a book a bonus star due to extenuating circumstances. If the writer is doing something new and exciting, for example. If they're trying something really difficult or if it's their first book, I'll often give an extra star. <br /><br />So. To the point. Did I enjoy this book? Yes. I didn't know there was a sequel to the Talisman until I saw this in an airport a week ago. I enjoyed reading it. Held my attention. Pleased me with its craft. <br /><br />Is it for everyone? No. So here's the breakdown. <br /><br /><br />** What I personally liked about this book:<br /><br />It was told in present tense, and done well. Not a lot of folks can pull that off. <br /><br />The narrator was almost an active character, almost like a tour guide through the story. He/she speaks directly to the reader at points, saying things like, "Let's see what's going on over at the old mill..." <br /><br />Again, it worked well. Extra points for that. <br /><br />Also, it was set in Wisconsin. Which is kinda fun for me. <br /><br /><br />**What you might like about this book:<br /><br />Everything that you normally like about Steven King's stuff. Interesting characters. Alternate worlds. Nice tie-in with the Talisman and the Dark Tower stuff. <br /><br />Nice description. Nice special effects. Nice tension and suspense. Nice characterization. <br /><br /><br />**What you might dislike about this book:<br /><br />It's a large, rambly story. A lot of the book is spent in atmospherics, developing non-essential characters, and digressions, rather than action and moving the story forward. <br /><br />The Talisman was a cool adventure story. A young boy goes out, explores a strange world on a quest to save his mom. <br /><br />This book isn't that. There's no real adventure. They don't even get into the other world until the last 80 pages or so. <br /><br />Children in danger. (I'm sensitive to this, having a kid now myself. It can be a dealbreaker for some folks.) <br /><br />Extreme potentially even gratuitous violence and gore. (But again, we're in the horror genre, so....) <br /><br />So there you go. Isn't that better than some arbitrary number of stars? Now you can make your own choice about whether you want to read it. Or not. It's up to you. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6254942034917126324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_170347003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170347003" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1239026-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484358p2/1239026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170347003?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/1239026-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170347003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer518900333922874955">(This review was originally published in the Washington Post in 2001.)<br/><br/><br/>Black House is a novel of slippage. We learn about slippage (a secondary definition of which, we are told, helpfully, in the text, is the feeling that things in general have just gotten, or will shortly get, worse) at the beginning of the book as we travel, invisibly through the town of French Landing, Wisconsin, early in the morning, winding up in an abandoned shack where “limp flypaper ribbons hung invisible within the fur o</span>
  <span id="freeText518900333922874955" style="display:none">(This review was originally published in the Washington Post in 2001.)<br /><br /><br />Black House is a novel of slippage. We learn about slippage (a secondary definition of which, we are told, helpfully, in the text, is the feeling that things in general have just gotten, or will shortly get, worse) at the beginning of the book as we travel, invisibly through the town of French Landing, Wisconsin, early in the morning, winding up in an abandoned shack where “limp flypaper ribbons hung invisible within the fur of a thousand fly corpses” and it is here that we encounter the mutilated body of ten-year-old Irma Freneau, and watch a dog attempt to eat her severed foot out from its running shoe.<br /><br />Irma is the latest victim of a serial killer whom the local paper has taken to calling the Fisherman, after Albert Fish, a real-life child-killer and cannibal. Not far from the shack, down a road, behind a no entry sign, is a house all painted black; and that house is a gateway to somewhere else.<br /><br />Slippage is what happens on the borders of things and places, and the town of French Landing is on many borders, one of which is the border between Stephen King country, and Peter Straub country.<br /><br />The plot itself will revolve around the struggle between two men: the murderous Fisherman, and our hero, Jack Sawyer, known locally as “Hollywood”, a retired homicide detective from LA. Jack Sawyer retired young and came out to Wisconsin in search of peace and quiet. It is a truism and a genre obligation that retired cops in novels, even novels with slippage, must come out of retirement for their last case, and Jack does, although, as we know from the off, this will not be a simple police procedural or even a whodunnit (the identity of the Fisherman is given to us early in the text -- the “hook of his nose” followed by the “wormy lips” are a dead giveaway, if we’ve missed the hints about his awful deeds and secret pleasures); and it will have its roots in a previous novel.<br /><br />Those who remember The Talisman, Straub and King’s first collaboration, have already met Jack Sawyer as a 12 year old boy who travelled a long way, across the US and across a distorted, magical version of America called the Territories, to find the Talisman that would save his dying mother’s life. The Talisman was a fantasy with dark elements: a fat book that could comfortably have been even fatter, with a winning young hero named after Tom Sawyer. <br /><br />Black House is a sequel of sorts to The Talisman, although it also draws upon the mythology that King has been building in his Gunslinger sequence, and which surfaced most recently in his Hearts in Atlantis. It is a book that exists on the borders of genre – it’s not a serial killer romance, although the Fisherman is unquestionably a superhuman serial killer possessed of (and by) strange powers. It is too dark to be a fantasy but too light, too deeply sunny, to be, at its heart, a horror novel. Here also we experience slippage.<br /><br />It can be a mistake to play hunt-the-author in any collaborative text. Collaborations work when two authors find a single voice for a story, and fail when they do not, and King and Straub create a mutual style that is clean and effective. It is knowing without being arch, and it does not read like either King or Straub. That there are dead giveaways in the text – the obscure jazz references that Straub delights in, for example, or some splattery scenes with a hedgeclipper that could only have been penned by King – is no help in the who-wrote-what game. (In fact I’d be willing to bet that most of the jazz references come from King, out to amuse his co-author and confuse reviewers, and that Straub took his turn at wielding the clipper.)<br /><br />Initially, I found Jack Sawyer uncomfortable in his role as the book’s hero as he is in his retirement: surrounded by a magnificent supporting cast of colourful characters, Jack comes off as almost too pure, too perfect; he might have wandered into this July Wisconsin-Hell-on Earth from a better place. But as I read on, I began to realise that in many ways Black House (only one vowel away from Bleak House, the foggy opening of which is quoted in the text) is a Victorian novel. The authors cited, quoted from, glossed, in the book are popular writers who once were read and are now both read and respected, particularly Dickens, Twain, and Poe. The characters, too, have a Dickensian quality to them. They are the forces of darkness – The Fisherman, Wendell Green the grasping newspaperman, Lord Malshun (Sauron as used-car salesman); forces of light – Jack Sawyer himself; Henry Leyden, the blind man with the many voices; the magnificently filthy brewer biker gang who call themselves the Hegelian Scum; brave Judy Marshall, who is being driven mad by her visions of the truth, and her son, Ty, who will become the Fisherman’s victim, and on whose rescue the fate of the universe, quite literally, depends. And the plot, which roller-coasters forward through the Wisconsin July, has the easy comfortable quality of something built by two authors who are perfectly well aware of how good they are, even to the point of referring to themselves as a couple of “scribbling fellows” in the text. (“Always scribble, scribble, eh Mr. King?”)<br /><br />Sometimes the collaborative process has its downside; on occasion the characters feel like counters being pushed back and forth across a board, and there is a final plot twist which smacks less of inevitability than it does of the authors checking off the last item on their to-do list. The use of the present tense, which could too easily get wearing over 600 pages, for the most part keeps the narrative voice supple, informal, and fresh, although it can, on occasion, make one feel as if one is reading a film script – and there is a sequence when Irma’s body is found, and the authors retread the same half hour from a number of points of view, in which it actively becomes a handicap. <br /><br />Such quibbles aside, in Black House one is watching two master-craftsmen, both at the top of their game, collaborating, with every evidence of enormous enjoyment, on a summery heartland gothic. The book is hugely pleasurable, and repays a reader in search of horror, adventure, or of any of the other joys, both light and dark, one can get from the best work of either of these two “scribbling fellows”.<br /><br />Whether King and Straub will reconvene for a final installment in another fifteen years, or whether Jack Sawyer’s tale has been subsumed into King’s Gunslinger series only time will tell. Either way, it is hard not to look forward to the eventual outcome.</span>
  <a data-text-id="518900333922874955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2819665790" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2819665790" />
    <a title="Baba" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60866073-baba"><img alt="Baba" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532961539p2/60866073.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2819665790?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Baba" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Baba" href="/user/show/60866073-baba">Baba</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780007100446" href="/book/show/65512.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60866073-baba?shelf=constant-readers">constant-readers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60866073-baba?shelf=horror-aiiiiiiiie">horror-aiiiiiiiie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2819665790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8052594157139673867">The weirdest thing about this and The Talisman is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6941.Peter_Straub' title='Peter Straub' rel='nofollow noopener'>Peter Straub</a>'s involvement, as the books are so completely entrenched in the Stephen King universe - especially this one!<br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597211357ra/29952324.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>The beams are breaking, the end of the world could be nigh, Roland has formed a new Ka-tet - meanwhile in French Landing someone is kidnapping, eating(!) and killing children and the local authorities and FBI have no clue; enter Mr 'all grown up' since <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/201449.The_Talisman' title='The Talisman by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Talisman</a>, and also a retired hero cop, Jack Sawyer!<br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597211357ra/29952323.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>A quintessential Step</span>
  <span id="freeText8052594157139673867" style="display:none">The weirdest thing about this and The Talisman is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6941.Peter_Straub" title="Peter Straub" rel="nofollow noopener">Peter Straub</a>'s involvement, as the books are so completely entrenched in the Stephen King universe - especially this one!<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597211357ra/29952324.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The beams are breaking, the end of the world could be nigh, Roland has formed a new Ka-tet - meanwhile in French Landing someone is kidnapping, eating(!) and killing children and the local authorities and FBI have no clue; enter Mr 'all grown up' since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/201449.The_Talisman" title="The Talisman by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a>, and also a retired hero cop, Jack Sawyer!<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597211357ra/29952323.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>A quintessential Stephen King (and Peter Straub) read - a small town is under siege by a monster living among them; there's an outsider (Sawyer) who some love and some hate; there's a White Hat with a disability; there's kids in peril... oh, and at least one dumb cop :). More horror focused than The Talisman, and more compelling with a freer flowing story, the Detective Columbo-esque approach of identifying the killer very very early on fits well in the context of this story. All in all, a very good read, further enhanced by huge Dark Tower ties. 8 out of 12<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1578852021ra/28764344.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_2132106085" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2132106085" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2132106085?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/539127.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=whodunit">whodunit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2132106085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12620575847000594325">Each time I pick up a Stephen King book, I am struck by the different writing voice I find. Truth is, I had expected it with Black House, sort of, being co-written with Peter Straub, and yet I was struck all the same. King/Straub narrate much of this tale from a moving bird’s-eye perspective, floating in and out of each character’s stance and location, with a twinge of humor on the side. They don’t even try to hide the fact that this account has been written in a book, by two writers even. I tho</span>
  <span id="freeText12620575847000594325" style="display:none">Each time I pick up a Stephen King book, I am struck by the different writing voice I find. Truth is, I had expected it with Black House, sort of, being co-written with Peter Straub, and yet I was struck all the same. King/Straub narrate much of this tale from a moving bird’s-eye perspective, floating in and out of each character’s stance and location, with a twinge of humor on the side. They don’t even try to hide the fact that this account has been written in a book, by two writers even. I thought this was funny, and I thought the style of writing was unlike many of the other King works before it. Believe me, I tried to deduce who wrote what. Was this King’s chapter, or was it Straub’s? “King-ism’s” came through of course. They always do. But overall, I could not tell. Then I found the reason why - while watching an interview of Stephen and his son Owen no less. Even they could not tell who had written what when looking back at their new book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34466922.Sleeping_Beauties" title="Sleeping Beauties by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Sleeping Beauties</a>. During their collaboration, they had edited and rewritten one other’s work along the way, thereby melding it. There was the answer.<br /><br />How did Black House fare compared to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13444.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_" title="The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a>? Pretty well, I’d say. Here’s the part where I admit that I cannot remember much of The Talisman. I am bummed about that, but just a little. Although I think it’s more than a good idea to read The Talisman first, it’s not going to kill the story if you happened not to. Black House looks back at the twelve year old Jack Sawyer and his quest across America, while creating a whole new chapter in the process. Jack is now an adult with no memory of the long ago journey to save his mom’s life. (Don’t worry Jack, I forgot too!) But his memory will return, and with it will come the recollection of that other world called The Territories. And as I read about his recollection, these words came through: <i>
  <blockquote>”There are other worlds than these.”</blockquote>
</i> That is not a line taken from Black House, although it could easily be one. The quote belongs to Jake Chambers from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43615.The_Gunslinger__The_Dark_Tower___1_" title="The Gunslinger (The Dark Tower, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gunslinger</a>. I bring this up because the story of Black House fits so well with The Dark Tower series. I don’t think there’s another book outside of the series that is closer to it than this one. The Talisman may be in some ways, but it seems to me that half the purpose of Black House is to tell a story of The Dark Tower that needed telling. Fine by me. Some of the things in those other worlds are a little bit “out there”, so to speak. Weird creatures. Wild ideas. And that’s fine too, cause the stories are always strong. Like this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12620575847000594325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17331014116445014706">Black House, Stephen King<br/>Black House is a horror novel by American writers Stephen King and Peter Straub. Published in 2001, it is the sequel to The Talisman. This is one of King's numerous novels, which also include Hearts in Atlantis and Insomnia, that tie in with the Dark Tower series. After the events of The Talisman, Jack Sawyer has repressed the memories of his adventures in The Territories and his hunt for the Talisman as a twelve-year-old boy, though the residue of these events has serve</span>
  <span id="freeText17331014116445014706" style="display:none">Black House, Stephen King<br />Black House is a horror novel by American writers Stephen King and Peter Straub. Published in 2001, it is the sequel to The Talisman. This is one of King's numerous novels, which also include Hearts in Atlantis and Insomnia, that tie in with the Dark Tower series. After the events of The Talisman, Jack Sawyer has repressed the memories of his adventures in The Territories and his hunt for the Talisman as a twelve-year-old boy, though the residue of these events has served to subtly affect his life even after he has forgotten them. Jack grew up to become a lieutenant in the Los Angeles Police Department, where his professionalism and uncanny talent have helped him establish a nearly-legendary reputation. When a series of murders in Los Angeles are traced to a farm insurance salesman from French Landing, Wisconsin, Jack cooperates with the French Landing Police to capture the killer. While in Wisconsin, Jack is irresistibly enraptured by the natural beauty of the Coulee Country, echoing his reaction to The Territories as a child. When he later intrudes upon a homicide investigation in Santa Monica, certain aspects of the crime scene threaten to revive his repressed memories. He subsequently resigns from the LAPD, and he moves to French Landing to enjoy his early retirement.<br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و نهم ماه می سال 2009 میلادی<br />عنوان: خانه سیاه؛ نويسنده: استیون کینگ؛ پیتر استراب؛ مترجم: کتایون نصیری مجد؛ ویراستار: علیرضا عامری؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، زهره، 1386، در 766 ص؛ شابک: 9789642981045؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی سده 21 م<br />ا. شربیانی</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1577884836" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1577884836" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1577884836?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345441034" href="/book/show/18129.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=politicalish">politicalish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=dark-tower">dark-tower</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=bad-shit-and-atrocities">bad-shit-and-atrocities</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=multi-dimensional">multi-dimensional</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=ebook_kindle">ebook_kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=mystery-and-thrillers">mystery-and-thrillers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1577884836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2223465027856725016">It's been a long time since I read this book. I remember reading it when it first came out, when I was in my late teens, and really enjoying it, but it's clear that I forgot sooo much about this book. I remember this one having more to do with the Dark Tower - but just how much, and in what detail surprised me. I almost wish that I hadn't read it now, that I'd waited until later on in my upcoming Dark Tower re-read with my bookclub, so I could read it at a more appropriate place within the Dark </span>
  <span id="freeText2223465027856725016" style="display:none">It's been a long time since I read this book. I remember reading it when it first came out, when I was in my late teens, and really enjoying it, but it's clear that I forgot sooo much about this book. I remember this one having more to do with the Dark Tower - but just how much, and in what detail surprised me. I almost wish that I hadn't read it now, that I'd waited until later on in my upcoming Dark Tower re-read with my bookclub, so I could read it at a more appropriate place within the Dark Tower timeline. <br /><br />But oh well. Sometimes one must live with the choices that are made. <br /><br />It's not really fair to call this book a sequel to The Talisman. I don't know what else to call it, because it IS that... but it sets the expectation that it will be similar to The Talisman, and it's not at all that. Talisman was an adventure, a quest, and yes, there was some dark and grim stuff in there (The Elroy Thing, Sunlight Gardener, Morgan &amp; Morgan, etc) and but it never felt hopelessly grim and depraved. This does. Not that I'm complaining, I loved it. But this is the evil twin that has been kept in the attic and fed fish heads making its appearance after 20 years. It came from the same stock, but it's not friendly or altogether sane. <br /><br />This is a dark book. It's a slow burn of a book. It's a character study book. It's a police-procedural-in-hell book. It's a Dark Tower book. It's a Stephen King book... so I don't know why anyone would expect anything less than the previous statements. <br /><br />We pick back up with Jack Sawyer 20 years after we left him, and he has made a career and a life for himself as a homicide detective. He's forgotten - or blocked - all memories of his previous quest to save his mother, and has tried to live as normal and mundane a life as possible, despite the fact that he had touched the Talisman, and it conferred a lasting good luck on him that made everything he wanted to do that much easier. However, Jack has pulled the plug on his career after seeing a black man murdered on the Santa Monica Pier and it dredges up some memories of Speedy Parker - and he gets the hell out of dodge. Moves to an idyllic little town in Wisconsin and never plans to be a cop again. Except of course for the fact that Ka is a wheel and he doesn't have a choice. Someone is killing children in his new hometown, and of course nothing is ever what it seems when King's writing. <br /><br />What follows is a grim, but excellent story that builds and builds. We encounter some old friends, make a lot of new ones (and more than a few enemies as well) and shit gets real. And surreal. <br /><br />I don't really want to talk too much about the plot or the story because it's best if one experiences it for themselves. It is a great book, and can stand alone - though you will get much more out of this if you are familiar with Jack's history. <br /><br />I loved most of the characters in this book, even the ones I detested. Wendell Green and Charles Burnside, I'm looking at you. In that order. Charles is what he is, and he does what he does and there's no punishment harsh enough for that. But for some reason, my disgust and rage really homed in on Green, and I felt like he was the most shitty person in this whole book. All of the other evils were evil, pure and simple. They wanted to kill and destroy and tear down the walls of the universe because that's what evil does. But fucking Wendell Green is just a shitty human who thinks way too highly of himself and is out for blood because he thinks that he's King Shit of Turd Hill - only nobody else recognizes that yet. He's the Rita Skeeter of French Landing. The shitty fucker. Shoulda been left for the Sisters. Just sayin'. <br /><br />ANYWHO... I loved Henry and Beezer and Mouse and Jack and Dale. All of these guys make me so proud of them. Even Bear Girl, in her small, small bit part, impressed me and kind of broke my heart. She's a rock, that girl. I would have liked a bit more about Mr. Munshun and The Big Combination. I wanted a bit more about what that was FOR. Obviously it was EVIL... but just what was it powering? <br /><br />Still... this is a great book, if a little tiny bit draggy at times. Like I said, it's a slow burner of a book. It takes a bit of patience, as often King's books do, but I think they pay off in the end. If I had ONE complaint, it would be the romantic element of the story. Ugh. Come on. It was unnecessary and so obvious and blah blah blah skim. Yeah, I get it, she's pretty... he's pretty... they are "interesting" and now instalove. Gag me. Leave out the instalove, and I'm golden. I could not care less. <br /><br />Otherwise, excellent stuff. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2223465027856725016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1769876680" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1769876680" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472073986p2/1642452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1769876680?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/1642452-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/539127.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1642452-kenny?shelf=stephen-king">stephen-king</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1769876680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10250625145173885155"><b><i>What you love, you must love all the harder because someday it will be gone.</i><br/>Black House ~~ Stephen King & Peter Straub</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1601708863i/30187302._SX540_.jpg' alt='1' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Last year, I finally made my first trip to Stephen King's universe by reading <b>THE TAILISMAN</b>. I wish I had read King's works in my teens. They would have been amazing trips for a 13 year old boy to take. Since visiting the Territories, I have journeyed to <b>'SALEM'S LOT</b> & now, French Crossing. I have many more trips planned with King. Once more, I was completely absorbed in the <b>worl</b></span>
  <span id="freeText10250625145173885155" style="display:none"><b><i>What you love, you must love all the harder because someday it will be gone.</i><br>Black House ~~ Stephen King &amp; Peter Straub</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1601708863i/30187302._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Last year, I finally made my first trip to Stephen King's universe by reading <b>THE TAILISMAN</b>. I wish I had read King's works in my teens. They would have been amazing trips for a 13 year old boy to take. Since visiting the Territories, I have journeyed to <b>'SALEM'S LOT</b> &amp; now, French Crossing. I have many more trips planned with King. Once more, I was completely absorbed in the <b>worlds</b> King created.<br><br>Stephen King &amp; Peter Straub collaborated on <b>THE TAILISMAN</b>, a story about the fantastic travels of a young boy named Jack Sawyer. In <b>BLACK HOUSE</b>, King and Straub tell the tale of the adult Jack. Wisely, they revisited Jack Sawyer decades later in life, making him a creature of this earth due to an understandable, but selective amnesia where the Territories are concerned. Exhausted by the horrors he's encountered as a successful homicide detective, Jack has retired ~~ quite young ~~ to the hoped-for peace and obscurity of small town Wisconsin life. He's found a dear friend in an older blind man ~~ an oddly elegant music buff who anonymously channels a handful of divergent personalities to whom radio listeners for miles tune religiously ~~ <i>think of him as Wolf in human form</i>. And now, Jack is beginning to feel at home in his new surroundings.<br><br>The older Jack is a fascinating &amp; complex protagonist. He is intelligent, compassionate, and confused, relying on intuition and luck to resolve conflicts. Once he understands what he must do, he never waivers, exuding confidence and leadership qualities around his companions. Jack’s numerous memories from the Territories, though rarely complete, set him apart from other humans. His fearlessness is both exaggerated and inspiring, yet he is impatient at times, losing his temper and hiding his past.<br><br>While <b>BLACK HOUSE</b> is considered to be a sequel to <b>THE TAILISMAN</b>. I’d be reluctant to describe it this way. The story-line is loosely connected to <b>THE TAILISMAN</b> in that it contains recurring characters ~~ Jack Sawyer &amp; Parker/Parkus ~~ &amp; features a few trips to the Territories but that's it. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1601708863i/30187303.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>BLACK HOUSE</b> uses an unusual &amp; distinctive narrative point-of-view. The story is initially written from the viewpoint of a crow ~~ a familar the serial killer calls Gorg that lures children for him ~~ so everything’s written as if you’re flying above the action and swoop into to take a closer look ~~ moving towards the sun, we glide away from the river. Once Jack enters the story, the narative changes &amp; the story becomes easier to follow.<br><br>Part of the brilliance of <b>BLACK HOUSE</b> is how it works on several levels ~~ murder mystery, character study, fantasy, horror story; it is both separate from &amp; bound to <b>THE TALISMAN </b>. <b>BLACK HOUSE</b> is an exciting read, &amp; quite scary as well. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1601708863i/30187304._SX540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>One of the most intreguing aspects of <b>BLACK HOUSE</b> is in its references to classic literature. <b>THE TALISMAN</b> relied heavily on the work of <i>Mark Twain</i>; here, King and Straub reference both <i>Poe</i> &amp; <i>Dickens</i>. The titles similarity to Bleak House is not incidental; the scarier sections of <b>BLACK HOUSE</b> parallels the foggy, damp descriptions of <i>Dickens</i>' ghost stories. Also similar to <i>Dickens</i> is the technique of establishing places, &amp; in which the sprawling cast of characters is introduced. With Jack being introduced late in the story, we grow attached to these people instead of being shown who they are in relation to Jack.<br><br><b>BLACK HOUSE</b> is a challenging, complex read ~~ much more challenging than <b>THE TALISMAN</b> &amp; <b>'SALEM'S LOT</b> . Readers are richly rewarded with an engrossing tale written by two masters at the top of their game.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1601708863i/30187305._SY540_.jpg" alt="1" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="10250625145173885155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2825580003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2825580003" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2825580003?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345441034" href="/book/show/18129.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2825580003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12019819233465845599">4.5 stars rounded up. Spoiler-free review. Spoiler discussion, Thursday Theorist episode, and spoiler-free review coming to YouTube this week.<br/><br/>Not being a fantasy fan, is it any wonder that I like this book more than its predecessor <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59219.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_' title='The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Talisman</a>? This one is far more engaging and interesting to me due to the characters and setting, not to mention it's written in one of my favorite styles: third-person omniscient. Not many books are written in this style anymore. An editor once told me it's becaus</span>
  <span id="freeText12019819233465845599" style="display:none">4.5 stars rounded up. Spoiler-free review. Spoiler discussion, Thursday Theorist episode, and spoiler-free review coming to YouTube this week.<br /><br />Not being a fantasy fan, is it any wonder that I like this book more than its predecessor <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59219.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_" title="The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a>? This one is far more engaging and interesting to me due to the characters and setting, not to mention it's written in one of my favorite styles: third-person omniscient. Not many books are written in this style anymore. An editor once told me it's because people don't like reading books written using that POV. I can dig it, but I've always enjoyed it when done right, and King and Straub did it right here.<br /><br />(Note: If you liked the intro and outro of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/107291.Needful_Things" title="Needful Things by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Needful Things</a> and wished there was an entire book written in that style, look no further, friends and neighbors.<br /><br />One of the first glaring differences you'll notice when jumping into this book (if you're as much of a fan of the craft as you are of stories) is that the book's written in present tense. This tense suits the omniscient POV, and lends an air of urgency to the proceedings. You rarely find present tense outside of the literary world these days, which is another shame. I've grown to enjoy it more than past tense over the years, and it seems that's going to be the theme of this review: liking antiquated/odd things.<br /><br />The third thing I noticed is how much our storytellers here have matured since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59219.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_" title="The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a>'s release. Their styles are mostly lost, despite a glimmer of King here and there, and I rarely caught myself saying, "Yup, so-and-so definitely wrote this part." There are passages that are 100% King, and quite a few that are no doubt Straub, but in the previous book those differences were everywhere. Every time the authors passed the baton in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59219.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_" title="The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a> I felt jarred, shaken, thrown out of sorts. Herein I only felt that way two or three times. <br /><br />I never once wanted to put this book down, whereas the previous book was a chore after the first 100 pages until the final 100 pages. Here, the book glides along, sometimes literally taking to the air to give the reader a god-like view of things. This is thanks to the third-person omniscient I noted in the first paragraph. <br /><br />I've stuck to reviewing the mechanics of this book because the story is a doozy and I fear talking about any piece of it would require a spoiler warning. Also, and probably more importantly, the mechanics were what I didn't like about the last book. All of that is fixed here, and I only removed half a star for some needless meandering during the scene at Ed's Eats. A tense scene felt less tense because of some obese narrative, but it's the only criticism I have.<br /><br />The popular consensus in the community is that if you liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59219.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_" title="The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a> you'll dislike <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10607.Black_House__The_Talisman___2_" title="Black House (The Talisman, #2) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Black House</a>, and I think that's fair. But I'd say the opposite is true, too. Hate <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59219.The_Talisman__The_Talisman___1_" title="The Talisman (The Talisman, #1) by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talisman</a>? There's a good chance you'll love this one. But that's just the opinion of a simple fanboy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12019819233465845599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9781451697735" href="/book/show/13573618-black-house">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5116650-janie-johnson?shelf=2013-challenge">2013-challenge</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer533416500" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer904580650536726959">Wow this book was, simply put, a mess and was very painful to read. Considering I totally enjoyed the Talisman, I expected this book to be just as mind capturing as its predecessor. I was very disappointed in just about the whole story. So it turned out to be 'just ok' for me.<br/><br/>The first half was drawn out and very wordy. The coming together of all the characters was quite confusing as well. It seemed to take half of the book before the story even got remotely interesting. <br/><br/>It would get good and th</span>
  <span id="freeText904580650536726959" style="display:none">Wow this book was, simply put, a mess and was very painful to read. Considering I totally enjoyed the Talisman, I expected this book to be just as mind capturing as its predecessor. I was very disappointed in just about the whole story. So it turned out to be 'just ok' for me.<br /><br />The first half was drawn out and very wordy. The coming together of all the characters was quite confusing as well. It seemed to take half of the book before the story even got remotely interesting. <br /><br />It would get good and then would settle back into the wordiness that I found throughout most of the story. Once it did finally reach its peak I was so exhausted, I just wanted the story to end. <br /><br />However, I did enjoy several of the characters in the story. So that was the highlight of the book. If not for them This may not even have reached the 2 star rating I gave it. <br /><br />At the end of the book it felt like it was left pretty wide open for more Of Jack and his adventures in the territories. All I can say about that is I hope King and Straub give us a better tale than they did with <i>Black House.</i><br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_26482763" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26482763" />
    <a title="Anthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/665259-anthony"><img alt="Anthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487534581p2/665259.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26482763?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anthony" href="/user/show/665259-anthony">Anthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26482763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8581268412844112332"><br/>I loved The Talisman. I think I read it when I was 13 or 14. It was a great story about a boy who could cross worlds, and took on a quest to save his dying mom. There were scary parts, but nothing too bad. Also, the line between King's writing and Straub's writing was pretty blurred. Either they shared the load, or one of them wrote while the other edited. I dunno, I could only hear one voice.<br/><br/>Years later, I listened to the audiobook and it was still great. <br/><br/>Black House is the sequel to The Talism</span>
  <span id="freeText8581268412844112332" style="display:none"><br />I loved The Talisman. I think I read it when I was 13 or 14. It was a great story about a boy who could cross worlds, and took on a quest to save his dying mom. There were scary parts, but nothing too bad. Also, the line between King's writing and Straub's writing was pretty blurred. Either they shared the load, or one of them wrote while the other edited. I dunno, I could only hear one voice.<br /><br />Years later, I listened to the audiobook and it was still great. <br /><br />Black House is the sequel to The Talisman. Now, I've been supping on a heavy diet of King over the past few months. I spent about six months listening to the Dark Tower books, and I took some time to fill in a few gaps in the story (I read Insomnia, Rose Madder and Everything's Eventual). So, Uncle Steve's voice is really strong in my head.<br /><br />The Black House is almost all Straub. I can pick out the King parts, but those are brief nuggets of good in a soup of bleh. I hate the way that the book reads like a screenplay. I hate the second-person POV. I hate the fact that the book isn't about Jack Sawyer. Not really. I listened to about 8 hours and then I stopped. I had tried to read the paper book, but stopped at 30 pages. I don't know how it ends, and that upsets me a little. But not enough to keep pushing soup around looking for chicken.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8581268412844112332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_121961090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121961090" />
    <a title="Dustin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4268487-dustin"><img alt="Dustin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595634356p2/4268487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121961090?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dustin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dustin" href="/user/show/4268487-dustin">Dustin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/539127.Black_House">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4268487-dustin?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4268487-dustin?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4268487-dustin?shelf=challenging-literature">challenging-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4268487-dustin?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121961090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10100316892034863195"><br/> In the early eighties, Stephen King and Peter Straub embarked on the ultimate coming-of-age tale. The Talisman easily solidified the collaboration's super status. Then, nearly two decades later, they returned to their literary roots. Black House portrays a different Jack Sawyer, now a semi-retired Los Angeles detective. He won't remain there much longer, though. By requesting his expertise in a major case, a colleague-turned-friend leads him to Wisconsin, where his life will be irrevocably alte</span>
  <span id="freeText10100316892034863195" style="display:none"><br /> In the early eighties, Stephen King and Peter Straub embarked on the ultimate coming-of-age tale. The Talisman easily solidified the collaboration's super status. Then, nearly two decades later, they returned to their literary roots. Black House portrays a different Jack Sawyer, now a semi-retired Los Angeles detective. He won't remain there much longer, though. By requesting his expertise in a major case, a colleague-turned-friend leads him to Wisconsin, where his life will be irrevocably altered...in numerous ways.<br /> <br /> Black House is very different from its predecessor. One of the most significant changes is the unique writing style. Almost everything's shown by way of what I like to call "an eagle's eye" view. This can be somewhat difficult and frustrating to adjust to, and I completely understand that critique. It's also a little slow at first. We're not actually reunited with Jack until the first 60 or so pages. <br /> My first time through the Coulee Country, I struggled with it a bit, too. But it being a King novel, I knew a big payoff was inevitable. <br /><br /> And maintaining his "I'm retired" mindset, Jack is reluctant to aid the local police investigation of a string of grisly serial killings. It's only until a young boy is abducted that Jack agrees to assist the authorities.<br /><br /> With the addition of a handful of eccentric characters (including the ever positive, delightful, and beloved Henry Lyden,) we're given recurring appearances of one or more characters from The Talisman.<br /> <br /><br /> One new addition, named Charles Burnside, alludes to a less than pleasant childhood, leaving something to be desired. I wanted to know more. For instance, how exactly was he mistreated (presuming, of course, that was the case,) what were his parents like? Who were his parents? What events helped form the individual shown throughout the novel?<br /> More importantly, can he be empathized with, knowing what we do about him? Should we be expected to? I felt next to nothing for him, whatsoever. Unless my utter abhorrence of him is put into consideration. That particular emotion resonates in every fiber of my being. But if I may return briefly to the aforementioned alluding, my heart does go out to him. Though all too fleeting...<br /> <br /> How about his time in Chicago? He displays an abundance of scorn which tells the reader of his pent-up resentment. What specifically happened there, though?<br /> That being said, I love the duality of ol' "burn, burn's" voices and/or accents. (In general, it's always a pleasure to find elements of duality in fiction, but in this case, I think King and Straub pulled it off exceptionally well.) Reminiscent of some nefarious-yet equally skilled- ventriloquist, the sequence baffles the mind in every sense of the word.<br /><br /> Additionally, I think I probably would have been more impacted if our killer had been less supernatural and more human. <br /> Why do I emphasize this point? Because, as of late, I've come to realize that villains who are more less fantastical (Rose Madder's Norman Daniels or The Shining's Jack Torrance, to name a few) have a much larger affect on me.<br /> <br /> I almost wish that the killer's identity had been withheld a bit longer. I believe if they'd done so, it would have created a much more suspenseful, biting-your-nails quality. Then again, the story's pretty dark and creepy. King and Straub probably weren't very interested in its mystery; contrarily, this story is very horror-orientated. <br /> <br /><br /> All throughout, a recurring theme is explored in interesting way(s:) repressed memories. This literary technique is seen in multiple characters, primarily our protagonist, Jack Sawyer.<br /> On a related note, scientific studies indicate that particularly traumatic experiences often result in repression, as a defense mechanism. And speaking personally, I'm a firm believer. I can recall very little of my childhood.<br /> I'm not the only one, either. King and Straub said it best: "Amnesia is merciful." Indeed.<br /><br /><br /> <br /> The final showdown (and the all-important journey toward that end,) felt slightly long-winded, but the psychological aspects almost demand it. As for the battle itself, I am torn. On the one hand, it is quite phenomenal. On the other, there's a comic book quality which renders it somewhat unrealistic. As a result, I'm left with many questions whose answers I'd be interested in learning. <br /><br /> Then, due to unforeseen events, Jack is inadvertently transported back to his past, so to speak. By taking their story in this direction, King and Straub present a few very suspenseful closing pages. I was literally holding my anxious breath and hoping for the best. I also realized the depth of my love and admiration for this amazing man. And through certain revelations, things are left open. There simply MUST be a 3rd book!!<br /><br /><br /> Jack's story isn't complete.<br /><br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="10100316892034863195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2522644539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2522644539" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/838641-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531438108p2/838641.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2522644539?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
